        <script type="text/javascript">
            function wpSolutionToggle(solution, link, showtext, hidetext) {
                if (solution.style.display != 'none') {
                    solution.style.display = 'none';
                    link.innerHTML = showtext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_show';
                } else {
                    solution.style.display = 'block';
                    link.innerHTML = hidetext;
                    link.className = 'solution_link_hide';
                }
            }

<script type="text/javascript">
//<![CDATA[



/* JQUERY */
jQuery(document).ready(function(){ 
    
   
  

	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish(); */
	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish().find('ul').bgIframe({opacity:false}); */
 
	/* For IE6 */
	if (jQuery.browser.msie && /MSIE 6\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 7\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 8\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent)) {

		/* Max-width for images in IE6 */		
		var centerwidth = jQuery("td#middle").width(); 
		
		/* Images without caption */
		jQuery(".post img").each(function() { 
			var maxwidth = centerwidth - 10 + 'px';
			var imgwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var imgheight = jQuery(this).height(); 
			var newimgheight = (centerwidth / imgwidth * imgheight) + 'px';	
			if (imgwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxwidth}); 
				jQuery(this).css({height: newimgheight}); 
			}
		});
		
		/* Images with caption */
		jQuery("div.wp-caption").each(function() { 
			var captionwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var maxcaptionwidth = centerwidth + 'px';
			var captionheight = jQuery(this).height();
			var captionimgwidth =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").width();
			var captionimgheight =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").height();
			if (captionwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxcaptionwidth}); 
				var newcaptionheight = (centerwidth / captionwidth * captionheight) + 'px';
				var newcaptionimgheight = (centerwidth / captionimgwidth * captionimgheight) + 'px';
				jQuery(this).css({height: newcaptionheight}); 
				jQuery("div.wp-caption img").css({height: newcaptionimgheight}); 
				}
		});
		
		/* sfhover for LI:HOVER support in IE6: */
		jQuery("ul li").
			hover( function() {
					jQuery(this).addClass("sfhover")
				}, 
				function() {
					jQuery(this).removeClass("sfhover")
				} 
			); 

	/* End IE6 */
	}
	
	
	
	/* Since 3.7.8: Auto resize videos (embed and iframe elements) 
	TODO: Parse parent's dimensions only once per layout column, not per video
	*/
	function bfa_resize_video() {
		jQuery('embed, iframe').each( function() {
			var video = jQuery(this),
			videoWidth = video.attr('width'); // use the attr here, not width() or css()
			videoParent = video.parent(),
			videoParentWidth = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'width' ) ),
			videoParentBorder = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-left-width' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-right-width' ) ),
			videoParentPadding = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-left' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-right' ) ),
			maxWidth = videoParentWidth - videoParentBorder - videoParentPadding;

			if( videoWidth > maxWidth ) {
				var videoHeight = video.attr('height'),
				videoMaxHeight = ( maxWidth / videoWidth * videoHeight );
				video.attr({ width: maxWidth, height: videoMaxHeight });
			} 

		});	
	}
	bfa_resize_video();
	jQuery(window).resize( bfa_resize_video );

		
	jQuery(".post table tr").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("over");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("over");
		});

	
	jQuery(".post table tr:even").
		addClass("alt");

	
	jQuery("input.text, input.TextField, input.file, input.password, textarea").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("highlight"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("highlight"); 
		})
	
	jQuery("input.inputblur").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("inputfocus"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("inputfocus"); 
		})

		

	
	jQuery("input.button, input.Button, input#submit").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("buttonhover");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("buttonhover");
		});

	/* toggle "you can use these xhtml tags" */
	jQuery("a.xhtmltags").
		click(function(){ 
			jQuery("div.xhtml-tags").slideToggle(300); 
		});

	/* For the Tabbed Widgets plugin: */
	jQuery("ul.tw-nav-list").
		addClass("clearfix");

		
	
});

//]]>
</script>

								<div class="post-52 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cpp-tutorial odd" id="post-52">
						<div class="post-headline">		<h1>2.10 &#8212; Chapter 2 comprehensive quiz</h1>
		</div>		<div class="post-byline">By Alex, on June 11th, 2007</div>		<div class="post-bodycopy clearfix"><p><strong>Quick Review</strong></p>
<p>Integers are used for holding whole numbers.  When using integers, keep an eye out for overflow and integer division problems.  Use fixed-width integers instead of the variable size integers.</p>
<p>Floating point numbers are used for holding real numbers (which can have fractional components).  When using floating point numbers, keep an eye out for precision issues, rounding errors, and comparison issues.</p>
<p>Boolean values hold only true and false.  They do not have any major issues.</p>
<p>Char values are integers that can be interpreted as an ASCII value.  When using chars, be careful not to mix up ASCII code values and numbers, and watch for overflow and integer division problems.</p>
<p>Use the const keyword to declare symbolic constants instead of #define.  It&#8217;s safer.</p>
<p><strong>Comprehensive quiz</strong></p>
<p><strong>Question 1</strong></p>
<p>Why are symbolic constants usually a better choice than literal constants?  Why are const symbolic constants usually a better choice than #defined symbolic constants?</p>
<p><strong>Question 2</strong></p>
<p>Pick the appropriate data type for a variable in each of the following situations.  Be as specific as possible.  If the answer is an integer, pick a specific integer type (e.g. int16_t) based on range.  If the variable should be unsigned or const, say so.  Favor signed numbers over unsigned numbers.</p>
<p>a) The age of the user (in years)<br />
b) Whether the user wants color or not<br />
c) pi (3.14159265)<br />
d) The number of pages in a textbook<br />
e) The price of a stock in dollars (to 2 decimal places)<br />
f) How many times you&#8217;ve blinked since you were born (note: answer is in the millions)<br />
g) A user selecting an option from a menu by letter<br />
h) The year someone was born</p>
<p><strong>Question 3</strong></p>
<p>Write the following program: The user is asked to enter 2 floating point numbers (use doubles).  The user is then asked to enter one of the following mathematical symbols: +, -, *, or /.  The program computes the answer on the two numbers the user entered and prints the results.  If the user enters an invalid symbol, the program should print nothing.</p>
<p>Example of program:</p>
<pre>
Enter a double value: 7
Enter a second double value: 5
Enter one of the following: +, -, *, or /: *
7 * 5 is 35
</pre>
<p>Hint: You can check if the user has entered a plus symbol using an if statement, covered in section <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/26-boolean-values/">2.6 -- Boolean values</a>.</p>
<p><strong>Question 4</strong></p>
<p>This one is a little more challenging.  Write a short program to simulate a ball being dropped off of a tower.  To start, the user should be asked for the initial height of the tower in meters.  Assume normal gravity (9.8 m/s<sup>2</sup>), and that the ball has no initial velocity.  Have the program output the height of the ball above the ground after 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 seconds.  The ball should not go underneath the ground (height 0).</p>
<p>Your program should include a header file named constants.h that includes a namespace called myConstants.  In the myConstants namespace, define a symbolic constant to hold the value of gravity (9.8 meters per second).  See section <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/2-9-symbolic-constants-and-the-const-keyword/">2.9 -- Symbolic constants and the const keyword</a> for a reminder on how to do this.</a></p>
<p>Use a function to calculate the height of the ball after x seconds.  The function can calculate how far the ball has fallen after x seconds using the following formula: distance fallen = gravity_constant * x_seconds<sup>2</sup> / 2</p>
<p>Sample output:</p>
<pre>
Enter the initial height of the tower in meters: 100
At 0 seconds, the ball is at height: 100 meters
At 1 seconds, the ball is at height: 95.1 meters
At 2 seconds, the ball is at height: 80.4 meters
At 3 seconds, the ball is at height: 55.9 meters
At 4 seconds, the ball is at height: 21.6 meters
At 5 seconds, the ball is at height: 0 meters
</pre>
<p><strong>Solutions</strong></p>
<p>1) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id1082213146'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id1082213146" style="display:none">
Using literal constants (aka. magic numbers) in your program makes your program harder to understand and harder to modify.  Symbolic constants help document what the numbers actually represent, and changing a symbolic constant at it&#8217;s declaration changes the value everywhere it is used.  #define constants do not show up in the debugger and are more likely to have naming conflicts.
</div>
<p>2) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id1213387618'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id1213387618" style="display:none">
a) int8_t wouldn&#8217;t be able to hold anybody who lived to be older than 127.  Although that&#8217;s not likely, it&#8217;s possible (or will probably be in the future).  So int16_t is probably the best choice.<br />
b) bool<br />
c) const double<br />
d) Since books can often have more than 255 pages but probably never have more than 32,767 pages, int16t is a good choice here.<br />
e) float<br />
f) int32_t<br />
g) char<br />
h) int16_t.  You can use positive numbers to represent AD birthdates, and negative numbers to represent BC birthdates.
</div>
<p>3) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id2115835429'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id2115835429" style="display:none">

<p>4) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id2046699500'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id2046699500" style="display:none">
In constants.h:<br />
<!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d77b195713061354402" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">

<p></p>
<p>Note that calculateHeight() doesn&#8217;t print the height itself, under the best practice that functions should do one and only one thing.  We use a different function to do the printing.

		<p>yea it&#8217;s good, but so simple at this point.  If I wanted to make that above program for real, it would need to be a bit more complicated. Who wants their program to just end? I would repeatedly remind the user to enter an appropriate choice until they did so.</p>
<p>I love giving wrong inputs to programs to see what happens.  Sometimes wrong inputs will lead to errors later on that could have been prevented.  Sometimes wrong inputs lead to invalid results.  if you are writing a game, the user could use wrong inputs to cheat. I say this to all you newbies to try to keep that in mind, and fix it when you learn how.  I am new to C++ and object oriented programming, but I learned on a Ti-83 mostly.  Good times.</p>

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=32956#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-32956", "32956", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Generally it&#8217;s not a good idea to write all your code and then compile it and see what happens.  It&#8217;s better to write a little bit, compile it, test it, fix the bugs, then repeat.  When your programs are relatively simple it doesn&#8217;t matter so much but as you move on to more complex things this will save you a lot of time.  Never too early to learn good habits. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=60824#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-60824", "60824", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Noha'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Char value can be used as integer. Give a look at the Quick Review in the beginning of this page.<br />
Because char occupy 1-byte which is 8 bits, the unsigned char variable has a range of 0 to 255. You can calculate it according to the formula that Alex wrote for unsigned variables: 0 to (2^n)-1, where n is the  number of bits.<br />
As you see, 255 is the maximal value for unsigned char variable taking into account that no body can live more. Only Noah lived more, so for him you should choose unsigned int <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>

		</div>
		<p>whats the difference between    +   &#8216;+&#8217;    and &#8220;+&#8221;</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-63181">
		<div id="div-comment-63181" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/91e5a597c4e7c35fe51eb3b832359c25?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/91e5a597c4e7c35fe51eb3b832359c25?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Eric</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-63181">
		June 26, 2009 at 12:23 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=63181#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-63181", "63181", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Eric'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>As far as I can tell, the difference is to differentiate between operations and characters.  + , for example, is used as an operator.  &#8216;+&#8217; is used when printing a character, as in question 4.  Characters are always written in single quotes, and putting them around the plus sign is done to make it easier for the programmer to identify it as a character and not as an operation.  </p>
<p>BUT, that&#8217;s just me.  I&#8217;ve been teaching myself C++ for the past 2 days now, so that that advice with a BIG grain of salt.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-3 clearfix" id="comment-251731">
		<div id="div-comment-251731" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251731">
		March 6, 2015 at 4:41 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251731#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251731", "251731", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>You&#8217;re on the right track.</p>
<p>+ can be both a unary or binary operator.  In unary form, it&#8217;s used to indicate a number is positive (which is redundant, because that&#8217;s assumed anyway).  In binary form, it&#8217;s used to add two operands together.</p>
<p>&#8216;+&#8217; is the ASCII character &#8216;+&#8217;, which is integer 43.</p>
<p>&#8220;+&#8221; is the string &#8220;+&#8221;.  We haven&#8217;t talked about strings too much yet, but it&#8217;s similar to &#8220;Hello, World&#8221;, except with different text.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		June 26, 2009 at 12:36 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=63183#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-63183", "63183", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Eric'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>If you&#8217;re talking about the characters (+, -, *, and /), then the answer is no.  Though those symbols look like operators, they&#8217;re being treated as characters when asking the user for their choice.  Since they are characters, they need the char declaration. None of those character are integers, so using any other declaration would not work. </p>
<p>I think that&#8217;s right&#8230;. I&#8217;m still learning too.</p>

		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251732">
		<div id="div-comment-251732" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-251732">
		March 6, 2015 at 4:47 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=251732#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251732", "251732", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>It won&#8217;t work if you use a different integer type (other than char) for chChoice.  This is because of the way cin interprets input.  If you&#8217;re inputting to a char variable, cin knows that an input of &#8216;+&#8217; should be treated as an ASCII value.  But if you&#8217;re inputting any other kind of integer, cin doesn&#8217;t know what to do with the +, so it fails.</p>
<p>BTW: This is something you could have figured out experimentally.</p>
		</div>

		<div class="comment-author vcard">

		December 2, 2009 at 7:43 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=73482#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-73482", "73482", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Kaonashi'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Answer to question 2a:<br />
<i>a) Since the oldest person alive is about 120 years old, a signed char would probably be okay. But since its possible that someone could live to be older than 127 (though not likely), an unsigned char would be even better.</i> </p>
<p>I answered unsigned int. Ages are whole numbers and can&#8217;t be negative, so a signed integer (-10) would be ridiculous; no one has a negative age. But why is the answer <b>char </b> instead of <b>int</b>?</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-75888">
		<div id="div-comment-75888" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/738f27894f853f564cefac9d4849f109?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/738f27894f853f564cefac9d4849f109?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Mulky</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-75888">
		January 6, 2010 at 4:08 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=75888#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-75888", "75888", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Mulky'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>I&#8217;m not 100% (as still learning myself) but I think the reason is to do with the amount of bytes they use and the size of the number they can hold. </p>
<p>Signed char is 1 byte in size, meaning the largest number it can hold is 127.<br />
Signed int is 4 bytes in size, meaning the largest number it can hold is 2,147,483,647.</p>
<p>So, as it is an age of a person the variable is going to record, a signed char would cover it as the odds of anyone living over 127 years are remote.</p>
<p>However, there&#8217;s no reason you couldn&#8217;t use an int, and I probably would, as seeing char in a program would make me think its got something to do with keeping track of ASCII codes.</p>
<p>I think though char is used as the answer as it gets the job done without being overkill (no one is ever going to be 2,147,483,647 years old) and the questions seem geared towards testing us to see if we know the number ranges of the different data types.</p>
<p>If I&#8217;ve missed the mark, I&#8217;d appreciate it if someone could set me straight <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-77861">

<p></p>
<p>This uses a &#8216;switch&#8217; statement instead of an &#8216;if&#8217; statement, but they are similar in function and if you mess around with that code you should be able to figure out how switches work.</p>

		<p>You used &#8220;+&#8221; instead of &#8216;+&#8217;.  &#8216;+&#8217; is a char, whereas &#8220;+&#8221; is a string, which has a different type.  You can&#8217;t compare a char with a string using operator==.</p>
		</div>

While I realize this program has a very simple result I&#8217;m trying to get in good coding habits. Here is my code, any suggestions? (Also I added a bit of functionality to the if statement after playing with it):<br />
<code><br />
 //Learncpp.com Ch2.10 Quiz question 4<br />
/*Program will take 2 floating point numbers from the user,<br />
ask them for an operator (+, -, *, /) and then output the<br />
result of the mathematical operation.*/</p>
<p>#include &quot;io.h&quot;</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	writeAnswer();</p>
<p>    return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>//I/O functions for the program</p>
<p>#include &lt;iostream&gt;</p>
<p>//Asks the user for the first number<br />
double firstNumber()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;</p>
<p>	//Get floating point number from user.<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter a number (can be a decimal): &quot;;<br />
	double dFirstNum;<br />
	cin &gt;&gt; dFirstNum;</p>
<p>	return dFirstNum;<br />
}</p>
<p>//Asks the user for the second number<br />
double secondNumber()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;</p>
<p>	//Get floating point number from user<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter another number (can be a decimal): &quot;;<br />
	double dSecondNum;<br />
	cin &gt;&gt; dSecondNum;</p>
<p>	return dSecondNum;<br />
}</p>
<p>//Asks the user for a mathematical operator<br />
char enterOperator()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;</p>
<p>	//Get a mathematical operator from user (+, -, *, /)<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter an operator (+, -, *, /): &quot;;<br />
	char	chOperator;<br />
	cin &gt;&gt;	chOperator;</p>
<p>	return chOperator;<br />
}</p>
<p>//Outputs the answer to the console<br />
void writeAnswer()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;</p>
<p>	//Get equation from user<br />
	double	dFirstNum	= firstNumber();<br />
	char	chOper		= enterOperator();<br />
	double	dSecondNum	= secondNumber();</p>
<p>	//Check to make sure the equation is valid and output the answer<br />
	if (chOper == &#039;+&#039;)<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; dFirstNum &lt;&lt; &quot; + &quot; &lt;&lt; dSecondNum &lt;&lt; &quot; = &quot; &lt;&lt; dFirstNum + dSecondNum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	else if (chOper == &#039;-&#039;)<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; dFirstNum &lt;&lt; &quot; - &quot; &lt;&lt; dSecondNum &lt;&lt; &quot; = &quot; &lt;&lt; dFirstNum - dSecondNum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	else if (chOper == &#039;*&#039;)<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; dFirstNum &lt;&lt; &quot; * &quot; &lt;&lt; dSecondNum &lt;&lt; &quot; = &quot; &lt;&lt; dFirstNum * dSecondNum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	else if (chOper == &#039;/&#039;)<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; dFirstNum &lt;&lt; &quot; / &quot; &lt;&lt; dSecondNum &lt;&lt; &quot; = &quot; &lt;&lt; dFirstNum / dSecondNum &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	else<br />
	{<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Invalid operator entry.&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
		writeAnswer();<br />
	}<br />
}</p>
<p>//I/O prototypes for program<br />
#ifndef IO_H<br />
#define IO_H</p>
<p>double	firstNumber();<br />
double	secondNumber();<br />
char	userOperator();<br />
void	writeAnswer();</p>
<p>#endif<br />
</code></p>
		</div>

		</div>
		<p>A couple of suggestions:<br />
1) Your main() function doesn&#8217;t do anything except call writeAnswer().  Since writeAnswer() isn&#8217;t reusable anyway, you might as well just put that code in main().<br />
2) firstNumber() and secondNumber() are somewhat redundant.  You could use the same function for both if you move the cout statements outside of the function.</p>

<p>#include </p>
<p>using namespace std;</p>
<p>int userinput(){<br />
	double x;<br />
	cout &lt;&gt; x;<br />
	return x;<br />
}  //gathers user input</p>
<p>int cOperate(){ //gather operation input<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Type in your operation please. / * + - :&quot; &lt;&gt; cOperation;<br />
	return cOperation;<br />
}</p>
<p>int ifstates (double x, char a, double y){<br />
	if (a == &#8216;/&#8217;){ //checks the operator<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; x / y &lt;&lt; endl;        // does the math<br />
	}<br />
	if (a == &#039;+&#039;){<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; x + y &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	}<br />
	if (a == &#039;*&#039;){<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; x * y &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	}<br />
	if (a == &#039;-&#039;){<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; x - y &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	}<br />
	if (a != &#039;-&#039;,&#039;+&#039;,&#039;/&#039;,&#039;*&#039;){<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; &quot;you did not input one of 4 operators&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	}<br />
	return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main(){<br />
	double num1 = userinput(); // user input number<br />
	char OP = cOperate();	   // user input character<br />
	double num2 = userinput(); // user input number<br />
	ifstates (num1, OP, num2); // checks to see what operator was inputed and does the math<br />
	system(&quot;pause&quot;);<br />
	return 0;<br />
system(&quot;pause&quot;);<br />
}</p>

#include<br />
#include </p>

<p>double getUserNumer()<br />
{<br />
	cout &lt;&gt; nY;<br />
	return nY;<br />

{<br />
	if (enterSymbol==&#8217;+&#8217;)<br />
		return nY + nX;<br />
	if (enterSymbol==&#8217;-&#8216;)<br />
		return nY - nX;<br />
	if (enterSymbol==&#8217;*&#8217;)<br />
		return nY * nX;<br />
	if (enterSymbol==&#8217;/&#8217;)<br />
		return nY / nX;<br />
	else<br />
		cout &lt;&lt; &quot;You did not input one of the four symbols provided, try again&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	return 0;<br />
}</p>

{<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The answer is: &quot; &lt;&lt; nResult &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
}</p>

{<br />
	double input1 = getUserNumer();</p>

		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/31fd3e47ec79b199b50140993ca29c74?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/31fd3e47ec79b199b50140993ca29c74?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">verit</span>

		December 21, 2013 at 7:44 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=114252#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-114252", "114252", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to verit'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>//2.0 Comprehensive Quiz question 4<br />
// Thought this would be a good program to use the &#8220;switch&#8221; statement.<br />
// Also attempted to use: enum op {+, -, *, /}; in various forms but learned that you can only enum // integers. Oh well&#8230;<br />
// Added an infinite &#8220;while()&#8221; loop to allow for greater testing. Use ctrl-c to break the loop.<br />
//</p>
<p>#include  </p>
<p>using namespace std; </p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	float num1, num2, answer;<br />
	char op;<br />
	while (1) {<br />
		cout &lt;&gt; num1;</p>
<p>		cout &lt;&gt; num2;</p>
<p>		cout &lt;&gt; op;<br />
		switch (op){<br />
			case &#8216;+':<br />
				answer = num1 + num2;<br />
				cout &lt;&lt; num1 &lt;&lt; &quot; + &quot; &lt;&lt; num2 &lt;&lt; &quot;is: &quot; &lt;&lt; answer &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
				break;</p>
<p>			case &#039;-&#039;:<br />
				answer = num1 - num2;<br />
				cout &lt;&lt; num1 &lt;&lt; &quot; - &quot; &lt;&lt; num2 &lt;&lt; &quot;is: &quot; &lt;&lt; answer &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
				break;</p>
<p>			case &#039;*&#039;:<br />
				answer = num1 * num2;<br />
				cout &lt;&lt; num1 &lt;&lt; &quot; x &quot; &lt;&lt; num2 &lt;&lt; &quot;is: &quot; &lt;&lt; answer &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
				break;<br />
			case &#039;/&#039;:<br />
				answer = num1 / num2;<br />
				cout &lt;&lt; num1 &lt;&lt; &quot; / &quot; &lt;&lt; num2 &lt;&lt; &quot;is: &quot; &lt;&lt; answer &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
				break;<br />
			default:<br />
				cout &lt;&lt; &quot;bad operator: &quot; &lt;&lt; op &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
			}</p>
<p>		}</p>
<p>	return 0;<br />
}</p>
		</div>

		<p>#include </p>
<p>using namespace std;</p>
<p>double userInput()<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Input a numerical value: &quot; &lt;&gt; x;<br />
    return x;<br />
}<br />
double calculation(double x, char y, double z)<br />
{<br />
    if (y == &#8216;+&#8217;)<br />
        return x + z;<br />
    if (y == &#8216;-&#8216;)<br />
        return x - z;<br />
    if (y == &#8216;*&#8217;)<br />
        return x * z;<br />
    if (y == &#8216;/&#8217;)<br />
        return x / z;<br />
    else<br />
        return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>int mathSymbol()<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter one of the following: +, -, *, /: &quot; &lt;&gt; x;</p>
<p>    return x;<br />
}</p>
<p>void printResult(double a, double b, char c, double d)<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; b &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; c &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; d &lt;&lt; &quot; is: &quot; &lt;&lt; a &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
    double input1 = userInput();<br />
    double input2 = userInput();<br />
    char chUserInput = mathSymbol();<br />
    double userResult = calculation(input1, chUserInput, input2);<br />
    printResult(userResult, input1, chUserInput, input2);</p>
<p>}</p>

		<p>Perhaps not the easiest way to write, but super easy to read what is happening when coded like this&#8230;</p>
<p>#include<br />
using namespace std; </p>
<p>int nReadNumber1()<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Please enter an integer.&quot; &lt;&gt; nInput1;<br />
    return nInput1;<br />
}</p>
<p>int nReadNumber2()<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Please enter a second integer.&quot; &lt;&gt; nInput2;<br />
    return nInput2;<br />
}</p>
<p>char chReadModifier()<br />
{<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter one of the following: +, -, *, or /.&quot; &lt;&gt; chInput3;<br />
    return chInput3;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
    // Get inputs<br />
    int nInputA = nReadNumber1();<br />
    int nInputB = nReadNumber2();<br />
    char chInputC = chReadModifier();</p>
<p>    // Determine modifier and do math<br />
    int nAnswer;<br />
    if (chInputC == &#8216;+&#8217;)<br />
        nAnswer = nInputA + nInputB;<br />
    else if (chInputC == &#8216;-&#8216;)<br />
        nAnswer = nInputA - nInputB;<br />
    else if (chInputC == &#8216;*&#8217;)<br />
        nAnswer = nInputA * nInputB;<br />
    else if (chInputC == &#8216;/&#8217;)<br />
        nAnswer = nInputA / nInputB;<br />
    else<br />
        cout &lt;&lt; &quot;You have entered an incorrect key.&quot; &lt;&lt; endl;</p>
<p>    //Write equation<br />
    cout &lt;&lt; nInputA &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; chInputC &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; nInputB &lt;&lt; &quot; = &quot; &lt;&lt; nAnswer &lt;&lt; endl;</p>
<p>    return 0;<br />
}</p>

<p></p>
<p>My queries:<br />
1) Does using float instead of double have any negative impact?<br />
2) I see you didn&#8217;t use static_cast, probably because the gravity itself is double, hence the whole expression will be evaluated to a double value. Is my understanding correct?</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-252099">
		<div id="div-comment-252099" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/#comment-252099">
		April 8, 2015 at 9:10 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=252099#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252099", "252099", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>1) You&#8217;ll have less precision using float, but for a trivial program like this, it probably doesn&#8217;t matter.  However, on general principle, I&#8217;d recommend always using double instead of float.  Unless you&#8217;re writing something performance intensive, there&#8217;s really no reason to give up the lack of precision to save a few bytes.</p>
<p>2) I didn&#8217;t use static_cast<double> because it wasn&#8217;t needed.  C++ will automatically convert integers into doubles as needed.  This is discussed in more detail in section <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/44-type-conversion-and-casting/" rel="nofollow">4.4 -- Type conversion and casting</a>.</double></p>
		</div>

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/210-comprehensive-quiz/?replytocom=252138#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252138", "252138", "respond", "52" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>

		<p>I agree, but I haven&#8217;t introduced loops yet.  Note that the problem specifies that we should tell the user where the ball is at 0, 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5 seconds, not however many seconds it takes until the ball reaches a height of 0.  That was to get around the lack of loops. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		<p>Looks fine.  The only thing I&#8217;d add is that you can use &#8216;+&#8217; instead of char(&#8216;+&#8217;), since the single quotes already indicate that + should be treated as a char.</p>

		<p>Anything done multiple times is a good candidate for a function.  You have a lot of repetitive calculations that would be better as a function.</p>
<p>It doesn&#8217;t really matter how many lines your program is.  What matters more is whether it produces correct results and is easy to understand.</p>

<p>#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
#include &lt;iostream&gt;</p>
<p>int totalValue(int dX, int dY, int z)<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;if (z == &#8216;+&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return (dX + dY);<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;-&#8216;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return (dX - dY);<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;*&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return (dX * dY);<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;/&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return(dX / dY);<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;using namespace std;</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter a number with decimals here: &quot;;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;double dX;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cin &gt;&gt; dX;</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter a second number with decimals here: &quot;;<br />

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Enter an operation here: &quot;;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;char z;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cin &gt;&gt; z;</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;bool operation(false);<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;if (z == &#8216;+&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;operation = true;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;-&#8216;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;operation = true;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;*&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;operation = true;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else if (z == &#8216;/&#8217;)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;operation = true;</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;bool finalStatement(operation);<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;if (finalStatement)<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; dX &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; z &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; dY &lt;&lt; &quot; is &quot; &lt;&lt; totalValue(dX, dY, z) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;else<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; endl;</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return 0;</p>
<p>}</p>
<p>Thanks in advanced~~~ :DDD for the awesome tutorial too</p>
